      <p><b>Qatar</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Qatar.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/En-us-Qatar.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Qatar.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Qatar-2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/En-us-Qatar-2.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Qatar-2.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">قطر</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Qaṭar</i></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈqatˤar]</a></span>; local vernacular pronunciation: <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈɡɪtˤɑr]</a></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-EoI_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> officially the <b>State of Qatar</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">دولة قطر</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Dawlat Qaṭar</i></i>), is a country located in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, occupying the small <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Qatar Peninsula">Qatar Peninsula</a> on the northeastern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC9Sep05_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC9Sep05-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USState2011_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USState2011-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ftManage_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ftManage-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ciaw_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciaw-18">[18]</a></sup> Its sole land <a href="/wiki/Qatar%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_border" title="Qatar–Saudi Arabia border">border</a> is with neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a> (GCC) monarchy <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to the south, with the rest of its territory surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bahrain" title="Gulf of Bahrain">Gulf of Bahrain</a>, an inlet of the Persian Gulf, separates Qatar from nearby <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>. </p>
      <p>In early 2017, Qatar's total population was 2.6 million: 313,000 Qatari citizens and 2.3 million <a href="/wiki/Expatriates" class="mw-redirect" title="Expatriates">expatriates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Official_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Official religion">official religion</a> of Qatar.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In terms of <a href="/wiki/Per_capita_income" title="Per capita income">income</a>, the country has the third-highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP (PPP) per capita</a> in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and the sixth-highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GNI_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GNI (nominal) per capita">GNI per capita (Atlas method)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Qatar is classified by the UN as a country of very high human development, having the third-highest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">HDI</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> after <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Qatar is a <a href="/wiki/High-income_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="High-income economy">World Bank high-income economy</a>, backed by the world's third-largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">natural gas reserves</a> and oil reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Qatar has been ruled by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Thani" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Thani">House of Thani</a> since <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Thani" title="Mohammed bin Thani">Mohammed bin Thani</a> signed a treaty with the British in 1868 that recognised its separate status. Following <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> rule, Qatar became a <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorate</a> in the early 20th century until gaining independence in 1971. In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Qatar" title="Constitution of Qatar">the constitution</a> was overwhelmingly approved in a referendum, with almost 98% in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-electionguide.org_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-electionguide.org-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-princeton.edu_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princeton.edu-26">[26]</a></sup> In the 21st century, Qatar emerged as a significant power in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> both through its globally expanding media group, <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera_Media_Network" title="Al Jazeera Media Network">Al Jazeera Media Network</a>, and reportedly supporting several rebel groups financially during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> For its size, Qatar wields disproportionate influence in the world, and has been identified as a <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/2022_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2022 FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup 2022</a> will be held in Qatar, making it the first Muslim and Arab country to host the event.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2030_Asian_Games" title="2030 Asian Games">2030 Asian Games</a> will be held in Qatar.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, a Roman writer, documented the earliest account pertaining to the inhabitants of the peninsula around the mid-first century AD, referring to them as the <i>Catharrei</i>, a designation which may have derived from the name of a prominent local settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qsahistory_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qsahistory-35">[35]</a></sup> A century later, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> produced the first known map to depict the peninsula, referring to it as <i>Catara</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-qsahistory_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qsahistory-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The map also referenced a town named "Cadara" to the east of the peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-katara_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-katara-37">[37]</a></sup> The term 'Catara' (inhabitants, <i>Cataraei</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> was exclusively used until the 18th century, after which 'Katara' emerged as the most commonly recognised spelling.<sup id="cite_ref-katara_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-katara-37">[37]</a></sup> Eventually, after several variations – 'Katr', 'Kattar' and 'Guttur' – the modern derivative <i>Qatar</i> was adopted as the country's name.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Standard_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Arabic">Standard Arabic</a>, the name is pronounced <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈqɑtˤɑr]</a></span>, while in the local dialect it is <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈɡitˤar]</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-12">[12]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Qatar" title="History of Qatar">History of Qatar</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg/220px-Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg/330px-Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg/440px-Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1148" data-file-height="766"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Excavated_site_on_Al_Khor_Island.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Excavation of a <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite</a> dye site on <a href="/wiki/Al_Khor_Island" title="Al Khor Island">Al Khor Island</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Human habitation of Qatar dates back to 50,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-cs1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs1-40">[40]</a></sup> Settlements and tools dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> have been unearthed in the peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-cs1_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs1-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> artifacts originating from the <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> (c. 6500–3800 BC) have been discovered in abandoned coastal settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-haya_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haya-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al_Da%27asa" title="Al Da'asa">Al Da'asa</a>, a settlement located on the western coast of Qatar, is the most important Ubaid site in the country and is believed to have accommodated a small seasonal encampment.<sup id="cite_ref-thaiembassy_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thaiembassy-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite</a> <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> material dating back to the second millennium BC found in <a href="/wiki/Al_Khor_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Khor Islands">Al Khor Islands</a> attests to trade relations between the inhabitants of Qatar and the Kassites in modern-day Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Among the findings were 3,000,000 crushed snail shells and Kassite potsherds.<sup id="cite_ref-thaiembassy_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thaiembassy-42">[42]</a></sup> It has been suggested that Qatar is the earliest known site of shellfish dye production, owing to a Kassite purple dye industry which existed on the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-haya_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haya-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 224 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> gained control over the territories surrounding the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Qatar played a role in the commercial activity of the Sasanids, contributing at least two commodities: precious pearls and purple dye.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Under the Sasanid reign, many of the inhabitants in Eastern Arabia were introduced to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> following the eastward dispersal of the religion by Mesopotamian Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-gillman_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillman-48">[48]</a></sup> Monasteries were constructed and further settlements were founded during this era.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> During the latter part of the Christian era, Qatar comprised a region known as 'Beth Qatraye' (<a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> for "house of the Qataris").<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The region was not limited to Qatar; it also included <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarout_Island" title="Tarout Island">Tarout Island</a>, Al-Khatt, and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">Al-Hasa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 628, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> sent a Muslim envoy to a ruler in Eastern Arabia named <a href="/wiki/Munzir_ibn_Sawa_Al_Tamimi" class="mw-redirect" title="Munzir ibn Sawa Al Tamimi">Munzir ibn Sawa Al Tamimi</a> and requested that he and his subjects accept Islam. Munzir obliged his request, and accordingly, most of the Arab tribes in the region converted to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> After the adoption of Islam, the Arabs<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2017)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> led the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a> which resulted in the fall of the Sasanian Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-fromherz_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fromherz-54">[54]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Early_and_late_Islamic_period_.28661.E2.80.931783.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_and_late_Islamic_period_(661–1783)">Early and late Islamic period (661–1783)</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Abbasids850.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/220px-Abbasids850.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/330px-Abbasids850.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/440px-Abbasids850.png 2x" data-file-width="1481" data-file-height="902"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Abbasids850.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> at its greatest extent, c. 850.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Qatar was described as a famous horse and camel breeding centre during the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-rahman_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rahman-55">[55]</a></sup> In the 8th century, it started benefiting from its commercially strategic position in the Persian Gulf and went on to become a centre of pearl trading.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kogan_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kogan-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Substantial development in the pearling industry around the Qatari Peninsula occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-rahman_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rahman-55">[55]</a></sup> Ships voyaging from <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> would make stops in Qatar's ports during this period. Chinese porcelain, West African coins and artefacts from Thailand have been discovered in Qatar.<sup id="cite_ref-fromherz_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fromherz-54">[54]</a></sup> Archaeological remains from the 9th century suggest that Qatar's inhabitants used greater wealth to construct higher quality homes and public buildings. Over 100 stone-built houses, two mosques, and an <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> fort were constructed in <a href="/wiki/Murwab" title="Murwab">Murwab</a> during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> When the caliphate's prosperity declined in Iraq, so too did it in Qatar.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Qatar is mentioned in 13th-century Muslim scholar <a href="/wiki/Yaqut_al-Hamawi" title="Yaqut al-Hamawi">Yaqut al-Hamawi</a>'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/Mu%27jam_Al-Buldan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mu'jam Al-Buldan">Mu'jam Al-Buldan</a></i>, which alludes to the Qataris' fine striped woven cloaks and their skills in improvement and finishing of spears.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Much of Eastern Arabia was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Usfurids" title="Usfurids">Usfurids</a> in 1253, but control of the region was seized by the prince of <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">Ormus</a> in 1320.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Qatar's pearls provided the kingdom with one of its main sources of income.<sup id="cite_ref-mohamed_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohamed-63">[63]</a></sup> In 1515, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_of_Portugal" title="Manuel I of Portugal">Manuel I of Portugal</a> vassalised the Kingdom of Ormus. <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> went on to seize a significant portion of Eastern Arabia in 1521.<sup id="cite_ref-mohamed_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohamed-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gillespie_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillespie-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1550, the inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">Al-Hasa</a> voluntarily submitted to the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, preferring them to the Portuguese.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Having retained a negligible military presence in the area, the Ottomans were expelled by the <a href="/wiki/Bani_Khalid" title="Bani Khalid">Bani Khalid</a> tribe in 1670.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Bahraini_and_Saudi_rule_.281783.E2.80.931868.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahraini_and_Saudi_rule_(1783–1868)">Bahraini and Saudi rule (1783–1868)</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Catura_(Qatar)_1794_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Map_of_Catura_%28Qatar%29_1794_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Map_of_Catura_%28Qatar%29_1794_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Map_of_Catura_%28Qatar%29_1794_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="259" data-file-height="203"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Catura_(Qatar)_1794_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 1794 map depicting <i>Catura</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Historical_region_of_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical region of Bahrain">Historical region of Bahrain</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1766, members of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Khalifa" title="House of Khalifa">Al Khalifa</a> family of the <a href="/wiki/Utub" class="mw-redirect" title="Utub">Utub</a> tribal confederation migrated from <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> to <a href="/wiki/Zubarah" title="Zubarah">Zubarah</a> in Qatar.<sup id="cite_ref-frauke_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frauke-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> By the time of their arrival, the Bani Khalid exercised weak authority over the peninsula, notwithstanding the fact that the largest village was ruled by their distant kin.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1783, Qatar-based <a href="/wiki/Bani_Utbah" title="Bani Utbah">Bani Utbah</a> clans and allied Arab tribes invaded and annexed <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> from the Persians. The Al Khalifa imposed their authority over Bahrain and retained their jurisdiction over Zubarah.<sup id="cite_ref-frauke_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frauke-67">[67]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Qatar,_Zubarah_(10),_ruined_city.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG/220px-Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG/330px-Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG/440px-Qatar%2C_Zubarah_%2810%29%2C_ruined_city.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1959" data-file-height="1469"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Qatar,_Zubarah_(10),_ruined_city.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A partially restored section of the ruined town of <a href="/wiki/Zubarah" title="Zubarah">Zubarah</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following his swearing in as crown prince of <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Diriyah" title="Emirate of Diriyah">the Wahhabi</a> in 1788, Saud ibn Abd al-Aziz moved to expand Wahhabi territory eastward towards the Persian Gulf and Qatar. After defeating the Bani Khalid in 1795, the Wahhabi were attacked on two fronts. The Ottomans and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptians</a> assaulted the western front, while the Al Khalifa in Bahrain and the <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Omanis</a> launched an attack against the eastern front.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qdl1_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qdl1-71">[71]</a></sup> Upon being made aware of the Egyptian advance on the western frontier in 1811, the Wahhabi amir reduced his garrisons in Bahrain and <a href="/wiki/Zubarah" title="Zubarah">Zubarah</a> in order to redeploy his troops. <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Sultan,_Sultan_of_Muscat_and_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Said bin Sultan, Sultan of Muscat and Oman">Said bin Sultan</a>, ruler of Muscat, capitalised on this opportunity and raided the Wahhabi garrisons on the eastern coast, setting fire to the fort in Zubarah. The Al Khalifa were effectively returned to power thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-qdl1_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qdl1-71">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As punishment for piracy, an <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> vessel bombarded <a href="/wiki/Doha" title="Doha">Doha</a> in 1821, destroying the town and forcing hundreds of residents to flee. In 1825, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Thani" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Thani">House of Thani</a> was established with Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Thani" title="Mohammed bin Thani">Mohammed bin Thani</a> as the first leader.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Although Qatar was considered a <a href="/wiki/Dependent_territory" title="Dependent territory">dependency</a> of Bahrain, the Al Khalifa faced opposition from the local tribes. In 1867, the Al Khalifa, along with the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a>, sent a massive naval force to <a href="/wiki/Al_Wakrah" title="Al Wakrah">Al Wakrah</a> in an effort to crush the Qatari rebels. This resulted in the maritime <a href="/wiki/Qatari%E2%80%93Bahraini_War" title="Qatari–Bahraini War">Qatari–Bahraini War</a> of 1867–1868, in which Bahraini and Abu Dhabi forces sacked and looted Doha and Al Wakrah.<sup id="cite_ref-Fromhertz2012_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fromhertz2012-73">[73]</a></sup> The Bahraini hostilities were in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_Truce_of_Peace_and_Friendship" title="Perpetual Truce of Peace and Friendship">Perpetual Truce of Peace and Friendship</a> of 1861. The joint incursion, in addition to the Qatari counter-attack, prompted British Political Resident, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Pelly" title="Lewis Pelly">Lewis Pelly</a> to impose a settlement in 1868. His mission to Bahrain and Qatar and the resulting peace treaty were milestones because they implicitly recognised the distinctness of Qatar from Bahrain and explicitly acknowledged the position of Mohammed bin Thani. In addition to censuring Bahrain for its breach of agreement, Pelly negotiated with Qatari sheikhs, who were represented by Mohammed bin Thani.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The negotiations were the first stage in the development of Qatar as a sheikhdom.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> However, Qatar was not officially recognised as a British protectorate until 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="The_Ottoman_period_.281871.E2.80.931915.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Ottoman_period_(1871–1915)">The Ottoman period (1871–1915)</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG/220px-Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="343" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG/330px-Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG 2x" data-file-width="411" data-file-height="641"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Qatar_in_Stielers_Handatlas_1891_59.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Qatar in an 1891 <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Stieler" title="Adolf Stieler">Adolf Stieler</a> map
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Olddoha2-771x410.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Olddoha2-771x410.jpg/220px-Olddoha2-771x410.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Olddoha2-771x410.jpg/330px-Olddoha2-771x410.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Olddoha2-771x410.jpg/440px-Olddoha2-771x410.jpg 2x" data-file-width="771" data-file-height="410"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Olddoha2-771x410.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Old city of Doha, January 1904.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Under military and political pressure from the governor of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Vilayet_of_Baghdad" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayet of Baghdad">Vilayet of Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midhat_Pasha" title="Midhat Pasha">Midhat Pasha</a>, the ruling <a href="/wiki/House_of_Thani" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Thani">Al Thani</a> tribe submitted to Ottoman rule in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-Rogan1199_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogan1199-77">[77]</a></sup> The Ottoman government imposed reformist (<a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a>) measures concerning taxation and land registration to fully integrate these areas into the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Rogan1199_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogan1199-77">[77]</a></sup> Despite the disapproval of local tribes, Al Thani continued supporting Ottoman rule. Qatari-Ottoman relations, however, soon stagnated, and in 1882 they suffered further setbacks when the Ottomans refused to aid Al Thani in his expedition of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a>-occupied <a href="/wiki/Khawr_al_Udayd" title="Khawr al Udayd">Khawr al Udayd</a>. In addition, the Ottomans supported the Ottoman subject Mohammed bin Abdul Wahab who attempted to supplant Al Thani as <a href="/wiki/Kaymakam" title="Kaymakam">kaymakam</a> of Qatar in 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> This eventually led Al Thani to rebel against the Ottomans, whom he believed were seeking to usurp control of the peninsula. He resigned as kaymakam and stopped paying taxes in August 1892.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In February 1893, Mehmed Hafiz Pasha arrived in Qatar in the interests of seeking unpaid taxes and accosting Jassim bin Mohammed's opposition to proposed Ottoman administrative reforms. Fearing that he would face death or imprisonment, Jassim retreated to Al Wajbah (16&nbsp;km or 10&nbsp;mi west of <a href="/wiki/Doha" title="Doha">Doha</a>), accompanied by several tribe members. Mehmed's demand that Jassim disbands his troops and pledge his loyalty to the Ottomans was met with refusal. In March, Mehmed imprisoned Jassim's brother and 13 prominent Qatari tribal leaders on the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvette</a> <i>Merrikh</i> as punishment for his insubordination. After Mehmed declined an offer to release the captives for a fee of 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Turkish_lira" title="Turkish lira">liras</a>, he ordered a column of approximately 200 troops to advance towards Jassim's <a href="/wiki/Al_Wajbah_Fort" title="Al Wajbah Fort">Al Wajbah Fort</a> under the command of Yusuf Effendi, thus signalling the start of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Al_Wajbah" title="Battle of Al Wajbah">Battle of Al Wajbah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fromherz_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fromherz-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Effendi's troops came under heavy gunfire by a sizable troop of Qatari infantry and cavalry shortly after arriving at Al Wajbah. They retreated to Shebaka fortress, where they were again forced to draw back from a Qatari incursion. After they withdrew to Al Bidda fortress, Jassim's advancing column besieged the fortress, resulting in the Ottomans' concession of defeat and agreement to relinquish their captives in return for the safe passage of Mehmed Pasha's cavalry to <a href="/wiki/Hofuf" title="Hofuf">Hofuf</a> by land.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Although Qatar did not gain full independence from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, the result of the battle forced a treaty that would later form the basis of Qatar's emerging as an autonomous country within the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="British_period_.281916.E2.80.931971.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="British_period_(1916–1971)">British period (1916–1971)</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Al_Zubarah_(6989829695).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg/220px-Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg/330px-Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg/440px-Al_Zubarah_%286989829695%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Al_Zubarah_(6989829695).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Zubarah_Fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Zubarah Fort">Zubarah Fort</a> built in 1938.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>By the Anglo-Turkish Convention of 1913, the Ottomans agreed to renounce their claim to Qatar and withdraw their garrison from Doha. However, with the outbreak <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, nothing was done to carry this out and the garrison remained in the fort at Doha, although its numbers dwindled as men deserted. In 1915, with the presence of British gunboats in the harbour, Abdullah bin Jassim Al Thani (who was pro-British) persuaded the remainder to abandon the fort and, when British troops approached the following morning, they found it deserted.<sup id="cite_ref-diwan_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwan-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Qatar became a British protectorate on 3 November 1916, when the United Kingdom signed a treaty with Sheikh Abdullah bin Jassim Al Thani to bring Qatar under its <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial System of Administration</a>. While Abdullah agreed not to enter into any relations with any other power without prior consent of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">British government</a>, the latter guaranteed the protection of Qatar from aggression by sea and provide its 'good offices' in the event of an attack by land – this latter undertaking was left deliberately vague.<sup id="cite_ref-diwan_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwan-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> On 5 May 1935, while agreeing an oil concession with the British oil company, the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Persian_Oil_Company" title="Anglo-Persian Oil Company">Anglo-Persian Oil Company</a>, Abdullah signed another treaty with the British government which granted Qatar protection against internal and external threats.<sup id="cite_ref-diwan_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwan-82">[82]</a></sup> Oil reserves were first discovered in 1939. Exploitation and development were, however, delayed by <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg/170px-British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg/255px-British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg/340px-British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="411"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:British_stamp_overprinted_for_use_in_Qatar_1_April_1957.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A British <a href="/wiki/Wilding_series" title="Wilding series">Wilding series</a> stamp, issued 1 April 1957, and overprinted for use in Qatar.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The focus of British interests in Qatar changed after the Second World War with the independence of India, the creation of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> in 1947 and the development of oil in Qatar. In 1949, the appointment of the first British political officer in Doha, John Wilton, signifed a strengthening of Anglo-Qatari relations.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Oil exports began in 1949, and oil revenues became the country's main source of revenue, the pearl trade having gone into decline. These revenues were used to fund the expansion and modernisation of Qatar's infrastructure. When Britain officially announced in 1968 that it would withdraw from the Persian Gulf in three years' time, Qatar joined talks with Bahrain and seven other <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial States</a> to create a federation. Regional disputes, however, persuaded Qatar and Bahrain to withdraw from the talks and become independent states separate from the Trucial States, which went on to become the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_and_aftermath_.281971.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_aftermath_(1971–present)">Independence and aftermath (1971–present)</span></h3> 
      <p>On 3 November 1916, the sheikh of Qatar entered into treaty relations with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The treaty reserved foreign affairs and defence to the United Kingdom but allowed internal autonomy. On 3 September 1971, those "special treaty arrangements" that were "inconsistent with full international responsibility as a sovereign and independent state" were terminated.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-88">[88]</a></sup> This was done under an agreement reached between the Ruler of Qatar and the Government of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-88">[88]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg/220px-Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg/330px-Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg/440px-Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="674"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Corniche_Doha_Qatar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">dhows</a> in front of the West Bay skyline as seen from the <a href="/wiki/Doha_Corniche" title="Doha Corniche">Doha Corniche</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1991, Qatar played a significant role in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, particularly during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Khafji" title="Battle of Khafji">Battle of Khafji</a> in which Qatari tanks rolled through the streets of the town and provided fire support for <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian_National_Guard" title="Saudi Arabian National Guard">Saudi Arabian National Guard</a> units that were engaging <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a> troops. Qatar allowed coalition troops from Canada to use the country as an <a href="/wiki/Canada_Dry_One" title="Canada Dry One">airbase</a> to launch aircraft on <a href="/wiki/Combat_Air_Patrol" class="mw-redirect" title="Combat Air Patrol">CAP</a> duty and also permitted air forces from the United States and France to operate in its territories.<sup id="cite_ref-cs1_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cs1-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1995, Emir <a href="/wiki/Hamad_bin_Khalifa_Al_Thani" title="Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani">Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani</a> seized control of the country from his father <a href="/wiki/Khalifa_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani" title="Khalifa bin Hamad Al Thani">Khalifa bin Hamad Al Thani</a>, with the support of the armed forces and cabinet, as well as neighbouring states<sup id="cite_ref-resh_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resh-90">[90]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nobs_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobs-91">[91]</a></sup> Under Emir Hamad, Qatar has experienced a moderate degree of <a href="/wiki/Liberalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberalisation">liberalisation</a>, including the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> television station (1996), the endorsement of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> or right to vote in municipal elections (1999), drafting its first written <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Qatar" title="Constitution of Qatar">constitution</a> (2005) and inauguration of a Roman Catholic church (2008). In 2010, Qatar won the rights to host the <a href="/wiki/2022_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2022 FIFA World Cup">2022 FIFA World Cup</a>, making it the first country in the Middle East to be selected to host the tournament. The Emir announced Qatar's plans to hold its first national legislative elections in 2013. They were scheduled to be held in the second half of 2013, but were postponed in June 2013 and <a href="/wiki/Next_Qatari_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="Next Qatari general election">may be delayed until 2019</a>. The legislative council will also host the 140th <a href="/wiki/Inter-Parliamentary_Union" title="Inter-Parliamentary Union">Inter-Parliamentary Union</a> Assembly for the first time in April 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2003, Qatar served as the US Central Command headquarters and one of the main launching sites of the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In March 2005, a <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide bombing</a> killed a British teacher from Dorset called Jonathan Adams<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> at the Doha Players Theatre, shocking the country, which had not previously experienced acts of terrorism. The bombing was carried out by Omar Ahmed Abdullah Ali, an Egyptian resident in Qatar who had suspected ties to <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Arabian_Peninsula" title="Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula">Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 2011, Qatar joined <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">NATO operations in Libya</a> and reportedly armed Libyan opposition groups.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> It is also currently a major funder of weapons for rebel groups in the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ft-20130516_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20130516-98">[98]</a></sup> Qatar is pursuing an Afghan peace deal and in January 2012 the Afghan <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> said they were setting up a political office in Qatar to facilitate talks. This was done in order to facilitate peace negotiations and with the support of other countries including the United States and Afghanistan. <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Rashid" title="Ahmed Rashid">Ahmed Rashid</a>, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i>, stated that through the office Qatar has "facilitated meetings between the Taliban and many countries and organisations, including the US state department, the UN, Japan, several European governments and non-governmental organisations, all of whom have been trying to push forward the idea of peace talks. Suggestions in September 2017 by the presidents of both the United States and Afghanistan have reportedly led to protests from senior officials of the American <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In June 2013, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani" title="Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani">Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani</a> became the Emir of Qatar after his father handed over power in a televised speech.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Sheikh Tamim has prioritised improving the domestic welfare of citizens, which includes establishing advanced healthcare and education systems, and expanding the country's infrastructure in preparation for the hosting of the 2022 World Cup.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Qatar participated in the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Houthis" class="mw-redirect" title="Houthis">Houthis</a> and forces loyal to former President <a href="/wiki/Ali_Abdullah_Saleh" title="Ali Abdullah Saleh">Ali Abdullah Saleh</a>, who was deposed in the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> uprisings.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The increased influence of Qatar and its role during the Arab Spring, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_protests_of_2011%E2%80%932014" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahraini protests of 2011–2014">Bahraini uprising in 2011</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Qatar–Saudi Arabia proxy conflict">worsened longstanding tensions with Saudi Arabia</a>, the neighboring <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> (UAE), and Bahrain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In June 2017, Egypt, Saudi Arabia, United Arab Emirates, and Bahrain <a href="/wiki/2017_Qatar_diplomatic_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 Qatar diplomatic crisis">cut off diplomatic relations</a> with Qatar, citing <a href="/wiki/Qatar_and_state-sponsored_terrorism" title="Qatar and state-sponsored terrorism">the country's alleged support of groups they considered to be extremist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-103">[103]</a></sup> This has resulted in increased Qatari economic and military ties with Turkey and Iran. </p>
      <p>Qatar is expected to host the <a href="/wiki/2022_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2022 FIFA World Cup">2022 FIFA World Cup</a> from 21 November to 18 December, becoming the first Arab country to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Qatar" title="Politics of Qatar">Politics of Qatar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_(Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani),_May_2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg/220px-Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg/330px-Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg/440px-Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_%28Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani%29%2C_May_2017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_meets_with_the_Emir_of_Qatar_(Sheikh_Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani),_May_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Emir <a href="/wiki/Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani" title="Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani">Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani</a> with U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> in May 2017.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Qatar is either a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional</a><sup id="cite_ref-BBC9Sep05_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC9Sep05-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USState2011_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USState2011-15">[15]</a></sup> or an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a><sup id="cite_ref-ftManage_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ftManage-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> ruled by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Thani" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Thani">Al Thani family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The Al Thani dynasty has been ruling Qatar since the family house was established in 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2003, Qatar adopted a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Qatar" title="Constitution of Qatar">constitution</a> that provided for the direct election of 30 of the 45 members of the Legislative Council.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lambert2011a_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lambert2011a-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reut20111101_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reut20111101-109">[109]</a></sup> The constitution was overwhelmingly approved in a referendum, with almost 98% in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-electionguide.org_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-electionguide.org-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-princeton.edu_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-princeton.edu-26">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The eighth Emir of Qatar is <a href="/wiki/Tamim_bin_Hamad_Al_Thani" title="Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani">Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani</a>, whose father <a href="/wiki/Hamad_bin_Khalifa_Al_Thani" title="Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani">Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani</a> handed power to him on 25 June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-New_Emir_BBC_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Emir_BBC-110">[110]</a></sup> The Emir has the exclusive power to appoint and remove the prime minister and cabinet ministers who, together, constitute the Council of Ministers, which is the supreme executive authority in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-embassycouncil_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embassycouncil-111">[111]</a></sup> The Council of Ministers also initiates legislation. Laws and decrees proposed by the Council of Ministers are referred to the Advisory Council (Majilis Al Shura) for discussion after which they are submitted to the Emir for ratification.<sup id="cite_ref-embassycouncil_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embassycouncil-111">[111]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Consultative_Assembly_of_Qatar" title="Consultative Assembly of Qatar">Consultative Assembly</a> has limited legislative authority to draft and approve laws, but the Emir has final say on all matters.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup> The current Council is composed entirely of members appointed by the Emir,<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup> as no legislative elections have been held since 1970 when there were partial elections to the body.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup> Legislative elections have been postponed until at least 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The council will also host the 140th <a href="/wiki/Inter-Parliamentary_Union" title="Inter-Parliamentary Union">Inter-Parliamentary Union</a> Assembly for the first time, in April 2019. </p>
      <p>Qatari law does not permit the establishment of political bodies or trade unions.<sup id="cite_ref-kuwari_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuwari-113">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3> 
      <p>According to Qatar's Constitution, <a href="/wiki/Sharia_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a> is the main source of Qatari legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-con_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-con-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qat1_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qat1-115">[115]</a></sup> although in practice, Qatar's legal system is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> and Sharia law.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Sharia law is applied to <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a>, and several <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal acts</a> (including adultery, robbery and murder). In some cases, Sharia-based <a href="/wiki/Family_court" title="Family court">family courts</a> treat a female's testimony as being worth half that of a man.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Codified family law was introduced in 2006. <a href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_Islam" title="Polygyny in Islam">Islamic polygyny</a> is permitted.<sup id="cite_ref-nobs_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobs-91">[91]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Judicial_corporal_punishment" title="Judicial corporal punishment">Judicial corporal punishment</a> is common in Qatar due to the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> interpretation of Sharia Law, although in Qatar it had originally been a <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> school of mainstream <a href="/wiki/Sunnism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunnism">Sunnism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Flogging" class="mw-redirect" title="Flogging">Flogging</a> is employed as a punishment for alcohol consumption or illicit sexual relations.<sup id="cite_ref-amne_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amne-119">[119]</a></sup> Article 88 of Qatar's criminal code declares that the penalty for adultery is <a href="/wiki/Flagellation" title="Flagellation">100 lashes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-flog_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flog-120">[120]</a></sup> and in 2006, a <a href="/wiki/Filipino_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Filipino people">Filipino</a> woman was sentenced that punishment.<sup id="cite_ref-flog_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flog-120">[120]</a></sup> In 2010, at least 18 people (mostly foreign nationals) were sentenced to receive between 40 and 100 lashes for offences involving "illicit sexual relations" or alcohol consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In 2011, at least 21 people (mostly foreign nationals) were sentenced to between 30 and 100 lashes for the same reasons,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and in 2012, six expatriates were sentenced to either 40 or 100 lashes.<sup id="cite_ref-amne_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amne-119">[119]</a></sup> Only Muslims considered medically fit are liable to have such sentences carried out. In April 2013, a Muslim expatriate was sentenced to 40 lashes for alcohol consumption,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> and in June 2014, a Muslim expatriate was sentenced to 40 lashes for consuming alcohol and driving under the influence.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Stoning" title="Stoning">Stoning</a> is a legal punishment in Qatar,<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> are crimes punishable by the <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty" class="mw-redirect" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a>, however, the death penalty hasn't been carried out for either.<sup id="cite_ref-iheu_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iheu-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">Blasphemy</a> can result in up to seven years in prison, while <a href="/wiki/Proselytizing" class="mw-redirect" title="Proselytizing">proselytising</a> can incur a 10-year sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-iheu_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iheu-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">Alcohol</a> consumption is partially legal in Qatar; some five-star luxury hotels are allowed to sell alcohol to their non-Muslim customers.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj20120107_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20120107-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arabist20120115_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arabist20120115-132">[132]</a></sup> Muslims are not allowed to consume alcohol, and those caught consuming it are liable to <a href="/wiki/Flogging" class="mw-redirect" title="Flogging">flogging</a> or deportation. Non-Muslim expatriates can obtain a permit to purchase alcohol for personal consumption. The Qatar Distribution Company (a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a>) is permitted to import alcohol and pork; it operates the one and only liquor store in the country, which also sells pork to holders of liquor licences.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Qatari officials have also indicated a willingness to allow alcohol in "fan zones" at the <a href="/wiki/2022_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2022 FIFA World Cup">2022 FIFA World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until 2011, restaurants on the <a href="/wiki/The_Pearl-Qatar" title="The Pearl-Qatar">Pearl-Qatar</a> (a man-made island near Doha) were allowed to serve alcoholic drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj20120107_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20120107-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arabist20120115_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arabist20120115-132">[132]</a></sup> In December 2011, however, Pearl restaurants were told to stop selling alcohol.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj20120107_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20120107-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-er6012012_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-er6012012-136">[136]</a></sup> No explanation was given for the ban,<sup id="cite_ref-wsj20120107_131-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20120107-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arabist20120115_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arabist20120115-132">[132]</a></sup> though speculation included encouraging a more pious image before a significant election and rumours of a financial dispute between the government and resort developers.<sup id="cite_ref-er6012012_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-er6012012-136">[136]</a></sup> The alcohol ban was later lifted.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2014, a modesty campaign was launched to remind tourists of the country's restrictive dress code.<sup id="cite_ref-'Leggings_Are_Not_Pants'_Qatar's_New_Modesty_Campaign_Aimed_At_Westerners_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-'Leggings_Are_Not_Pants'_Qatar's_New_Modesty_Campaign_Aimed_At_Westerners-138">[138]</a></sup> Female tourists were advised not to wear leggings, miniskirts, sleeveless dresses, or short or tight clothing in public. Men were warned against wearing only shorts and singlets.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Qatar" title="Foreign relations of Qatar">Foreign relations of Qatar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_(40723423453).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg/220px-Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg/330px-Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg/440px-Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_%2840723423453%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5473" data-file-height="3649"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Secretary_Pompeo_Meets_With_Qatari_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_Sheikh_Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_Al_Thani_(40723423453).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Deputy Prime Minister and Minister of Foreign Affairs <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Abdulrahman_bin_Jassim_Al_Thani" title="Mohammed bin Abdulrahman bin Jassim Al Thani">Mohammed bin Abdulrahman bin Jassim Al Thani</a> with U.S. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pompeo" title="Mike Pompeo">Mike Pompeo</a> in 2019.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As a small country with larger neighbours, Qatar seeks to project influence and protect its state and ruling dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-Boghardt_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boghardt-140">[140]</a></sup> The history of Qatar's alliances provides insight into the basis of their policy. Between 1760 and 1971, Qatar sought formal protection from the high transitory powers of the Ottomans, British, the Al-Khalifas from Bahrain, the Arabians, and the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabis</a> from Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2013)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Qatar's rising international profile and active role in international affairs has led some analysts to identify it as a <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a>. Qatar was an early member of <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> and a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a> (GCC). It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>. The country has not accepted compulsory <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Embassy_of_Qatar,_Washington,_D.C..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg/220px-Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg/330px-Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg/440px-Embassy_of_Qatar%2C_Washington%2C_D.C..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Embassy_of_Qatar,_Washington,_D.C..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Embassy of Qatar in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Qatar also has bilateral relationships with a variety of foreign powers. Qatar is a strategic ally of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, with relationship between the two countries growing stronger.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> It also hosts the <a href="/wiki/Al_Udeid_Air_Base" title="Al Udeid Air Base">Al Udeid Air Base</a>, a joint U.S.-British base, which acts as the hub for all American and British air operations in the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> It has allowed American and British forces to use an air base to send supplies to Iraq and Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> According to leaked documents published in <i>The New York Times</i>, Qatar's record of counter-terrorism efforts was the "worst in the region".<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20130630_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20130630-146">[146]</a></sup> The cable suggested that Qatar's security service was "hesitant to act against known terrorists out of concern for appearing to be aligned with the U.S. and provoking reprisals".<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20130630_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20130630-146">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Qatar has mixed relations with its neighbours in the Persian Gulf region. Qatar signed a defence co-operation agreement with Iran,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> with whom it shares the largest single non-associated gas field in the world. It was the second nation, the first being France, to have publicly announced its recognition of the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_opposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Libyan opposition">Libyan opposition</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Transitional_Council" title="National Transitional Council">National Transitional Council</a> as the legitimate <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Libya" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Libya">government</a> of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> amidst the <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Libyan civil war">2011 Libyan civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2014, Qatar's relations with <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> came to a boiling point over Qatar's support for the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a><sup id="cite_ref-resh_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resh-90">[90]</a></sup> and reportedly funding extremist groups in Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> This culminated in the three aforementioned countries withdrawing their ambassadors from Qatar in March 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-Islam_Hassan_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_Hassan-150">[150]</a></sup> Qatar was also involved in the CIA-led <a href="/wiki/Timber_Sycamore" title="Timber Sycamore">Timber Sycamore</a> covert operation to train and arm <a href="/wiki/List_of_armed_groups_in_the_Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="List of armed groups in the Syrian Civil War">Syrian rebels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg/220px-Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg/330px-Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg/440px-Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Qatar_in_Libya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Qatar's flag in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> after the <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 Libyan Civil War">Libyan Civil War</a>; Qatar played an influential role during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In recent years, Qatar has been using Islamist militants in a number of countries including Egypt, Syria, Libya, Somalia and Mali to further its foreign policy. Courting Islamists from the Muslim Brotherhood to <a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafist</a> groups has served as a power amplifier for the country, as it believes since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> that these groups represented the wave of the future.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20130630_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20130630-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boghardt_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boghardt-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> David Cohen, the Under Secretary for terrorism and financial intelligence at the U.S. Treasury, said that Qatar is a "permissive jurisdiction for terrorist financing."<sup id="cite_ref-ISIS_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISIS-153">[153]</a></sup> There is evidence that these groups supported by Qatar include the hard-line Islamic militant groups active in northern Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20130630_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20130630-146">[146]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Qatar&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Qatar, Saudi Arabia and Turkey are openly backing the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Conquest" title="Army of Conquest">Army of Conquest</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> an umbrella group of anti-government forces fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil
